WATCH: VP Vance gives remarks in Des Moines, IA

LiveNOW from FOX(5.6M) ·36:15 ·10.7K views · 2026-05-05
JD Vance (Vice President) Zach Nunn (US Representative, Iowa)

TL;DR Vice President JD Vance advocated for the re-election of Congressman Zach Nunn, framing the 2026 election as a fundamental choice between a government that prioritizes American workers through manufacturing protection and anti-fraud measures versus a Democrat party he...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance advocated for the re-election of Congressman Zach Nunn, framing the 2026 election as a fundamental choice between a government that prioritizes American workers through manufacturing protection and anti-fraud measures versus a Democrat party he characterizes as prioritizing illegal immigration and corporate corruption.

Economic Growth and Manufacturing Protection

  • Domestic Tax Policy: Vance highlighted the "Working Families" tax cuts as a primary driver for local business expansion.
      • According to factory owners, these tax breaks allowed for higher bonuses, increased salaries, and the creation of new jobs in the Des Moines area.
  • Trade and Tariffs: The Vice President emphasized a "common sense" approach to trade that penalizes foreign "dumping."
      • The China Factor: Vance criticized past administrations for allowing China to use "slave labor" to undercut Iowa workers.
      • Tariff Strategy: He reaffirmed the administration's stance that foreign countries attempting to steal American jobs must pay a "big fat tariff" to access U.S. markets.
  • Housing and Cost of Living: Vance claimed that for the first time in five years, rent costs have decreased for eight consecutive months.
      • Policy Drivers: He attributed this shift to principles favoring American family homeownership over foreign investors and "Chinese investors buying up farmland."
      • Immigration Link: He argued that sealing the border would lower housing costs by reducing the demand for 30 million homes currently occupied by illegal immigrants.

Federal Accountability and the Fraud Task Force

  • Anti-Fraud Initiatives: As the leader of the federal fraud task force, Vance described the government as "fishing in a barrel with dynamite" regarding the discovery of misappropriated funds.
  • SNAP Program Abuse: Agriculture Secretary Brooke Rollins has identified significant mismanagement within the Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program (SNAP).
      • Fact: 186,000 deceased individuals are currently receiving food stamps.
      • Fact: 355,000 people are receiving double benefits.
      • Wealth Disparity: Investigations found individuals owning luxury vehicles, such as Lamborghinis, while remaining on the SNAP rolls.
  • International Fraud: Vance detailed a case in Minneapolis where funds intended for parents of children with autism were diverted to "Somalian fraudsters" via illegal immigrant networks.
      • The Federal Response: The administration has threatened to cut off federal funding to states like Minnesota unless they conduct rigorous audits of benefit recipients.

Agricultural Expansion and Biofuels

  • Export Market Growth: Vance reported that the administration has doubled the export market for Iowa farmers in just 18 months.
      • This was achieved by leveraging trade authorities to demand market access for Iowa products in exchange for lower tariffs on foreign goods.
  • Energy Independence (E15): The Vice President pledged continued support for Congressman Nunn’s legislation to expand E15 ethanol access nationwide.
      • Goals: Increasing profit margins for Iowa farmers while lowering fuel costs for consumers across all 50 states.
  • Input Costs: Acknowledged the strain of high fertilizer prices and committed to working on domestic supply chain solutions to alleviate these costs for farmers.

Political Contrast and "Earning the Sacrifice"

  • Congressional Matchup: Vance contrasted Congressman Zach Nunn with his opponent, Sarah Trone Garriott, focusing on their legislative priorities.
      • He criticized Garriott’s voting record on social issues—such as gender transition for minors and transgender participation in sports—arguing these topics distract from the economic needs of the district.
  • Party Realignment: Vance, who noted he was raised in a "union Democrat" household, argued the modern Democrat party has abandoned working-class families in favor of "insider stock trading" and illegal immigrant advocacy.
  • The "Earn This" Standard: Referencing a meeting with Gold Star families in Des Moines, Vance closed with a call to make the government worthy of the ultimate sacrifice made by service members.
      • The Burden of Service: He noted that Iowa has borne a heavy burden in recent Middle East operations, losing a significant percentage of those killed in action.
      • Political Duty: He defined "earning" that sacrifice as sending representatives to Washington who prioritize the American citizen's "sweat, tears, and time."

Notable Quotes

"Do you want people in Washington, D.C. who fight for you, who fight for the people of this district, or who fight for corruption and fraud?" — Vice President JD Vance
"Finding fraud in the federal government—it's kind of like fishing in a barrel with dynamite." — Vice President JD Vance
"I actually think that we should take food stamps away from dead people. I am guilty of that. I think that's a reasonable thing." — Vice President JD Vance
"Part of how we earn that incredible sacrifice given to all of us is by making this country's politics and government worthy of the people who put on the uniform and will never see their loved ones again." — Vice President JD Vance

That was an amazing, probably the best introduction I think I've ever gotten in my life. But most importantly, I want to thank him for being a good fighter for the people of this district. When you send somebody to Congress, you're never ever going to agree with every single thing that they do, but you deserve to know that when they're in the halls of power—and especially when the cameras are off—they're thinking about you, about your families, and about your future. A guy that you have serving this district, Congressman Nunn, is one of those guys who does the right thing not just when the cameras are on, but when the cameras are off, too. Okay, he's got the same cold that our other friend did. Steven Bradley—where's Steven Bradley? Thank you, Steven. And finally, Mark Cisneros. Mark, thank you so much for your leadership and thanks for being here. Look, I'm here today to talk a little bit about the stakes in November of 2026. Because I think that we have done a lot in Washington, D.C. to be proud of. We're going to talk about it. But the fundamental question for us in this election is actually not any specific question of public policy; it's not any particular issue. It's fundamentally: do you want people in Washington, D.C. who fight for you, who fight for the people of this district, or who fight for corruption and fraud? Because I'm 41 years old, and for the first time in my entire life, I think we have seen a major political party in the United States of America completely go off the rails. They're not just talking about different views on taxes, or on regulation, or on the Clean Water Act, as important as all those things are. They're talking about who the government actually stands and fights for. When the President of the United States asked the entire Congress to stand for the simple principle that the people's government ought to fight for the American people first, what did we see? We saw every single congressional Democrat sit on their hands. They didn't care about you; they didn't care about the people of this district; they didn't care about the farmers or the factory workers or the people who actually make this country run. Now we have in Washington, D.C. a Democrat party that is so focused on illegal immigration, so focused on people who don't have the legal right to be here, and so focused on fraud because so many of their friends get rich from fraud, that they forgot to look after you. Whatever your political views—whether you're a sensible Republican, an independent, or even a sensible Democrat, because I know we've got a lot of those in Iowa, if not in Washington, D.C.—I think that you deserve to have a President of the United States and a Congressman like Zach Nunn who fights for you every single day. That's what he's been doing. Now let me just give you a very concrete example of what fighting for you looks like. This is not just abstract; this is not words written on a paper. This is real people's lives, and this is real dollars and cents. A few years ago, I talked to the owners of this factory, who told me the great products that they were making. They were paying their workers a good wage and they were building a great business right here in Iowa. You know, this company was started in 2009, the year that I graduated from college. Sorry for anybody, that makes me kind of young. But here's the thing: to start a business in 2009 was a tough thing to do. It was the middle of the global financial crisis. You had to take some real risk. And what did this business find after building a great business and after paying hundreds of people a solid wage? They found that foreign countries, in particular the People's Republic of China, were dumping goods, undercutting the wages of the workers of this factory, and driving this place out of business. They were using cheap labor—sometimes slave labor—to try to steal jobs from the hardworking people of this district and this factory floor. For my entire life, we had presidents and politicians of both parties who didn't do a single thing to stop it. They said, "Oh, by the way, absolutely, bring those cheap goods into our country. Bring those things that were built by hands of slaves rather than the hands of our own workers." Of course, we knew that when they drove those factories out of business, then they'd come in and jack up the price right after. So what happened? For 41 years, we all know the story. The factories closed down. My hometown—this is the story of my hometown where I grew up in a community that was proud, where people built things and where new homes were being made. By the time I graduated from high school, the manufacturers were laying people off. Those big, beautiful factories were shuttering their doors. And we never had a political movement that said, "Enough is enough," until Donald J. Trump and Zach Nunn came along. The Democrats every day will tell you they will fight against our policies to try to bring back American manufacturing. They will fight against our policies that try to fight back against those people who would dump that stuff into our communities. And you know what we say? We say that our tax policy ought to be pretty simple, and that's exactly what it has been. If you're building right here in Iowa, if you're making something right here in Iowa, if you're working every single day right here in Iowa, you deserve a tax cut, and that's exactly what we delivered with Congressman Nunn. But if you're a foreign country dumping that garbage into our country, if you're trying to undercut the wages of Iowa workers, or if you're trying to steal the jobs from Iowa farmers and Iowa manufacturers, you know what you're going to do? You're going to pay a big fat tariff before you bring anything into our country because we're protecting the jobs of our workers and our people for a change. That's all this is about. When you hear the Democrats attack the Trump administration's tax policies, that's what they're attacking. They're attacking the policies that make it harder for people to steal your jobs, and they're attacking the policies that give you more money to keep in your own pocket. I talked to the factory owners again just a couple of hours ago—a couple minutes ago, I should say. You know what they told me? They told me that the working families tax cuts, the tax cuts made possible by Congressman Nunn, meant that their business had a lot more money they could give to workers in higher bonuses and higher salaries. They could hire more people; they could expand and create new jobs that didn't exist before. Isn't it nice to have a government that finally is fighting for you instead of fighting against you? That's what we're trying to have in Washington, D.C. Now there is so much I'm proud of, so much that is moving in the right direction. Do you know when I was going around the campaign trail in 2024, one of the number one things that young people said to me—young Americans in particular—is that they couldn't afford to buy a home? They couldn't afford their rent, if that's what they wanted to do. For the first time we've seen in over five years, for eight months running, we have seen the cost of rent come down in the United States of America. Our young people are starting to get close. They're making progress. They're starting to get to the point where they can finally afford their slice of the American dream. And you know why we did that? You know how we did it? It's actually not that complicated. It is a simple principle: American homes ought to belong to American families, not foreign investors, not foreign owners, and not Chinese investors who are buying up our farmland. Again, it's not rocket science; it's a simple principle that if you have a government that fights for you, it can make your life easier. It can make it easier for you to start a business or to work at an amazing job. We didn't do anything that was particularly difficult. We did something that was simple and common sense. I think that's what defines the Republican party in Washington right now: it's simply about common sense. You want to lower the cost of homes for American families? Here's a simple thing. Why don't you stop giving 30 million homes to illegal aliens? Why don't you seal the border and make sure those homes go to American families for a change? That's how you lower the cost of housing. If you want to make it easier for young Americans to start a family or to get a good job, stop giving jobs to foreign companies and stop giving jobs to foreign countries. Protect the good, well-paying jobs that exist right here in the United States of America, and that's what we did. And here's the final thing. When we talk about standing for you—again, whatever your views on a specific policy—there is maybe no more important issue than when you pay your taxes to the federal government. We just had tax day a couple of weeks ago. How about you have a government that actually takes those resources as what they are: your sweat, your tears, and your time away from your family, and protects every single one of those dollars? That's what we're doing by going after fraud finally in the federal government. Let me just give you a couple of examples. We had let fraud become so rampant in this country that people were able to get rich not by creating something amazing, not by employing someone, and not by building something beautiful with their hands. They were able to get rich by defrauding every single person in this room. They were taking money that should go to America's low-income families, to America's elderly, and to people who are struggling in our communities. They were stealing it out of their pocket and stealing it out of your pocket so they could get rich. Let me give you just a couple of examples. There was a program in suburban Minneapolis—it's actually a national program but it was taken advantage of in suburban Minneapolis—where we make it possible for the parents of young children who are autistic to get access to after-school services. I think that's a cool thing because as Americans, we take care of one another. But you know what happened? That money wasn't going to the parents of autistic children; that money was going to Somalian fraudsters. Somebody figured out in the illegal immigrant community that if they went around and said all of the children in a particular household had that particular problem, then they could collect a lot of money from the federal government. So what happened? You paid your taxes, those people got rich, and the families who needed those services never got to access them. They were stolen from and you were stolen from, and your government didn't do a single thing about it. Until, that is, Donald J. Trump and Congressman Nunn came along. What we did is very simple. We went to the state of Minnesota and said, "We want you to audit the people who are getting access to these funds. And if you're not willing to confirm that the people getting these benefits actually deserve these benefits, then you're not getting another dime of federal money." We cut them off, and that's exactly what we got to do. Ladies and gentlemen, people have asked me—you know, I'm the leader of the fraud task force in Washington—and people have asked me, "Is that a hard job?" And I say, "Well, let me think. Finding fraud in the federal government is kind of like fishing in a barrel with dynamite." Every week I get a report from the task force—we have our weekly meeting—and I am shocked every single day by the things that we find. Just one of the all-stars on our fraud task force is our great agriculture secretary, Brooke Rollins. Brooke has found out that we've got 355,000 people on SNAP benefits receiving double benefits. We've got 186,000 dead people getting SNAP benefits. 186,000 dead Americans are getting food stamps right now. Now, I know the fake news media is going to say that the big headline from this speech is "JD Vance proposes that we take away food stamps." That's what they're going to say. I actually think that we should take food stamps away from dead people. I am guilty of that. I think that's a reasonable thing. I also was talking to Brooke backstage and she said—and this is not a joke—she said, "You know that there are people who have Lamborghinis who currently receive SNAP benefits." I said, "Huh. That's interesting." I'm a pretty conservative guy, but I would think that like 95 percent of the United States of America would agree that if you are wealthy enough to afford a Lamborghini, then you are wealthy enough to not receive SNAP benefits from the American people. Is that common sense? I could go down and down the list. We just served 22 investigatory procedures with the Department of Justice. We went from discovering what happened in Minneapolis to serving 22 subpoenas so that we could go after the fraud that's happening in Minneapolis. And we did that in just three months. And yes, ladies and gentlemen, when we find crime, those people are going to go to prison for defrauding you, because that's exactly how it should be. Now, the last thing I want to talk about, my friends, is that we know under the Biden administration, Iowa farmers and farmers all over the United States of America were hit, and they were hit hard. What we've done using the President's trade authorities and using his incredible trade policies is that we have doubled the export market for Iowa farmers in just 18 months, and that is an amazing thing. What happened for years is that people would dump their agricultural products into the United States of America but said, "You're not allowed to sell great Iowa product overseas." Well, we flipped that on its head. We told all those countries, "You're done dumping that crap into the United States of America, and if you don't want to pay a higher tariff rate, you better let Iowa farmers sell into your country." And every single one of them have let Iowa farmers sell into their country. Again, it's amazing what happens when you've got a president and a congressman who are fighting for you instead of fighting against you, and that is the record that we have had over the past 18 months. So let me say a couple more things here. I was flipping around in my notes here because I've been trying to find the name of the person that Zach is running against because we're going to make fun of her a little bit. Now, here is the problem. I don't know anything about her. What I do know is her voting record. I know what she has chosen to prioritize, and it's not you, it's not your families, and it's not this district. There are problems and there are challenges. One of the things I'm proudest of is that under the President's leadership, we had American families about $1,300 richer from the day that he took office until today. That's between the working families tax cuts, lowering the cost of rent, and all that stuff combined. Now that's the good news. Let me give you the bad news. The honest piece of this is that under the Biden administration, the average American family lost about $3,000. So on the one hand, we've got a great record to brag on. On the other hand, because of the hole that the Biden administration and the Democrats put us in, we've got a lot more work to do, okay? But we recognize that work. We're excited about that work. That's why you sent us to Washington, D.C. That's why you sent Zach to Washington, D.C.—to work on these problems. We know right now that we've got a lot of Iowa farmers that would love to sell more E15 into the market, and we're fighting every day to help them. Now, I said that and I did not even see the E15 crew we've got over here! I need one of those t-shirts. The congressman has got legislation that's working right now and we're working on it every single day. Because number one, we want Iowa farmers to have access to more markets, but number two, we want all of our fellow citizens across our 50 great states to benefit from what's made right here in Iowa. That's why we're fighting for E15. We also know that a lot of our farmers are struggling with high fertilizer prices. I'm aware of that. As the President of the United States has said, we've got a little blip in the Middle East and we've got to take care of some business on the foreign policy side, but do you know what we're doing simultaneously? We're working with the congressman and we're finding ways every single day to make sure that you guys and the farmers of this great state get access to the products that they need. We're working on it every single day. When I see Iowa farmers who need to get that E15 to market, what does—Zach, you're going to have to help me out with her name here. I lost my page here. Okay, there we go. Sarah Trone Garriott, there she is. I'm on the wrong page here. While Zach is fighting to make sure for the benefit of Iowa, and frankly for the benefit of the whole country, that we get more E15 into the market all over the United States of America, his opponent voted to push biological males into girls' sports as early as kindergarten. While he fought very hard for the working families tax cut to make sure that you got to keep more of your hard-earned money—which is why you guys, in fact, are starting to have the biggest tax refund season in American history—he was fighting for more money in your pocket, and she was voting against banning gender transition surgeries for minors. She was voting against schools giving parental notification if students start changing their pronouns. Now, we all hear that and we all think that's pretty crazy. But recognize that not everybody agrees with us. Even if you don't think that's crazy, why is that the priority of a woman who wants to represent you in Washington, D.C.? Why isn't she working to increase your take-home pay? Why isn't she fighting for the farmers to get more of that E15 all over the United States? Why isn't she fighting to lower housing costs for the people of this district, the people of this state, and the people of the United States of America? It goes back to that question that I asked at the very beginning of this discussion. We have two parties in Washington, D.C. I hate to say this because I was raised by blue-collar union Democrats. My mamaw and papaw said that when they were voting in the '80s and '90s, they were for Democrats because Democrats are for working people. I don't think that's true anymore, my friends. And it's a heartbreaking thing. It's heartbreaking for a kid who came from a union Democrat family to realize that Democrats these days seem to care more about gender transition than they do about you keeping more of your hard-earned money. I really do believe this: there are Democrats like my mamaw and papaw all over the great state of Iowa, but I hate to say it and I hate to break it to you, but there are none left in Washington, D.C. The Democrats in Washington, D.C. want to trade stocks—insider trading for money—just like Nancy Pelosi did. The best part of the State of the Union address was when the President of the United States called out Nancy Pelosi for her stock trading. I looked over at her and she had the sourest look I have ever seen on a human being's face. It turns out Nancy Pelosi likes nothing less than being called out for her insider stock trading. You know, we were just talking about that at the State of the Union. We had this beautiful six-year-old girl—you may remember this, it was a few months ago, but it was a very revealing moment about who our respective political parties stand for. This little girl had been shot by an illegal alien. But she had survived; miraculously, she was in amazing condition. She was a sweet, just a really cute kid. And you know, I'm the father of a six-year-old boy; he's actually here with me, he's backstage. This girl was so proud. She was proud to be there in the chamber and she gave her dad a big hug, and the Republicans all stood and cheered, and the Democrats all sat on their hands. I remember thinking to myself: what is wrong with you that you cannot stand up and cheer for a six-year-old girl who survived an assassin's bullet? What is wrong with you that you cannot vote to give more money to the pockets of Iowa farmers and Iowa factory workers? What is wrong with you when you cannot stand up for the Americans—some of whom literally have taken bullets in the United States of America—but you fight every single day for illegal immigrants who have no right to be in the United States in the first place? And what does it say about a political party that they have fought more against our anti-fraud efforts than they ever did to protect your dollars and to protect the benefits of the people who use them? My friends, this is not a normal election. This is not a normal political environment. This is a contest between a party that wants to take all of your money and give it to illegal aliens and a contest between gentlemen like Zach Nunn who fight every single day for you. I have a simple question here: do you want somebody who votes to give your money to illegal aliens? No! Do you want somebody who votes to raise your taxes? No! Do you want somebody who supports defrauding you and taking money away from poor kids so they can give it to the fraudsters? No! Then do we want crazy far-left Democrats in Washington, D.C.? No! Here's what we do want. Do we want somebody who fights for you every single day? Yes! Do we want somebody who has put more money in your pocket? Yes! Do we want somebody who gives E15 all over the United States of America so Iowa farmers can profit from it? Yes! So what do we want? We want Congressman Zach Nunn, and you've got to get him to Washington, D.C. So let me just say one more thing, my friends, and then I will hit the road. Like I said, I've got my six-year-old son back there. And the backstory of why I started in Washington, D.C.—we flew from Washington to Ohio. I voted in Ohio's primary today. That's right. The media asked me who I voted for—none of your business! Went from Ohio to Oklahoma City to do a fundraiser for the Republican National Committee and then I came here to be with all of you this evening. I can't wait to tell my wife when we get home that we've watched about nine episodes of X-Men and my son has eaten four chocolate chip cookies. She's going to be very pleased; she's going to be very pleased with the vice president. But here's the thing: it kind of just worked out. I wasn't planning this, but I've got my six-year-old because last week I took his little sister on a trip and he was feeling a little jealous. You know, when he's a kindergartener, it's not like missing school—no offense—but one day of kindergarten is not going to cause him any problems. So he came out with me today and we've had such a good day. It's just been an amazing thing. I always enjoy coming out and doing events like this, but to do it with your six-year-old son by your side is really an amazing thing. But as it happened, when we landed here in Des Moines at the airport, we met a couple of Gold Star families from Iowa. A couple of you probably know this—maybe all of you know this—that in what's going on in the Middle East, this operation in Iran, Iowa has borne a heavier burden maybe than any other state in the Union. This great state has lost about half of our KIA, and I know that you all will join me in saying that we love them, and we're proud of them, and we're praying for them every single day. Thank you. On the tarmac, a couple of Gold Star families met me—a couple of families who've lost people just in the last nine or so months. One in Iran and then one in Syria about nine or so months ago. I was talking to them and one of them told me—I had no idea, I didn't plan to see them—but they were here and of course I was honored to see them. It's an amazing thing as the Vice President of the United States to meet the parents and the families of the people who put on a uniform and sacrificed their lives for this country. I'm always happy to do it, always honored to do it. But one family—and I won't use their name, I don't know how political they are, if they're political at all—told me that their son's birthday was today. And so I get in the car with my little six-year-old and I look into those big eyes—his eyes remind me of his mother's eyes—and I think to myself: what an amazing blessing it is to be able to be a father in the freest and most prosperous country anywhere in the history of the world. I thought to myself: what would I say if this beautiful six-year-old boy got older and decided, like his dad, to put on the uniform of his country and, like so many of you here, decided to put on the uniform of his country? On the one hand, I'd be so proud of him. I'd be so proud of him. But on the other hand, I'd be so terrified that what happened to those two families would happen to this boy. It hit me that every time we lose somebody, every time someone goes overseas and sacrifices their life, every time that a person gives the ultimate sacrifice to the United States of America, we often read about it as a line in a newspaper or online. But there's a father, and there's a mother, and there's a grandma, and aunts and uncles, and a whole crew of people who love them the same way that we all love every single member of our family. You take that love and you set that on one side, and you consider the balance that all of us owe. On the other side is that we have to meet their sacrifice by carrying something forward that they can't carry forward. We have to make this country worthy of that sacrifice. You remember that movie *Saving Private Ryan*? That's one of my favorite movies. At the very end, right before one of the soldiers takes his last breath, he looks up and he says, "Earn this." What he means is all that love, all that was taken away from those families, all that was taken away from so many of the people who have put on the uniform and sacrificed for this country—we have to earn it. So much of that is how you live your life. So much of that is, you know, Congressman Zach Nunn being a good husband and father in his beautiful family here. I think—what, you guys have six or seven? Six. She is a saint, Zach. She is a saint. Part of that is how we go to work and treat our employees and what we build and make with our hands and with our minds. Part of it is how we go to church and give back to our community. But part of how we earn that incredible sacrifice given to all of us is by making this country's politics and government worthy of the people who put on the uniform and will never see their loved ones again. Part of what we have to do, my friends, is every single day we have to send people to Washington—I'm not saying you have to agree with every single issue—I'm saying that you've got to send people to Washington who wake up every single day and recognize that they fight for you. That's how we honor this sacrifice. That's how we make this country great again. And that's how we make 250 years of American history and American patriots proud. God bless you guys and thank you for having me in Iowa.

Stanford AI Club: Chamath on How to Win in the AI Era

Stanford AI Club(18.1K) ·54:23 ·14.7K views · 2026-05-05
Chamath Palihapitiya (Co-founder, Social Capital)
Chamath Palihapitiya argues that the next phase of AI value creation lies in building a "control plane" that translates English-language business secrets and symbolic logic into deterministic code, moving beyond simple pattern matching to solve complex, long-horizon enterprise tasks.
TL;DR Chamath Palihapitiya argues that the next phase of AI value creation lies in building a "control plane" that translates English-language business secrets and symbolic logic into deterministic code, moving beyond simple pattern matching to solve complex, long-horizon...

TL;DR

Chamath Palihapitiya argues that the next phase of AI value creation lies in building a "control plane" that translates English-language business secrets and symbolic logic into deterministic code, moving beyond simple pattern matching to solve complex, long-horizon enterprise tasks.

The 8090 Thesis: Bridging the Symbolic and Embedded Spaces

  • The AI Maturity Gap: Current AI models are "fancy autocompletes" that predict tokens based on patterns rather than thinking from first principles. Consequently, they fail at complex problems and long-horizon tasks.
  • The Symbolic Control Plane: Palihapitiya’s company, 8090, aims to build a "honeypot" for the symbolic space—the English-language requirements, PRDs, and tribal knowledge that define a company’s success.
      • English as the Universal Code: English is the unifying language for eight billion people; innovation will shift from technical algorithm design to "judgment" and "ideas" expressed in plain language.
      • The Software Factory: 8090 uses a "software factory" model where humans collaborate with models to bind English requirements to engineering plans.
      • Legacy Modernization: This approach allows companies to reverse-engineer millions of lines of legacy code (e.g., COBOL) into English-language "secrets" that current executives can actually understand and manipulate.
  • Avoiding the Trough of Disillusionment: To justify the trillions of dollars being invested in AI, companies must prove a massive ROI through 50%+ productivity gains. This requires rebuilding how companies operate from the "studs" up.

Organizational Design: The "Infinite Game" and Radical Efficiency

  • Finite vs. Infinite Games: Palihapitiya defines success as moving from finite games (competing for scarce resources like promotions) to infinite games (practicing a craft to prove something to oneself).
  • The 8090 Operating Model:
      • Eliminating Hierarchy: The company operates without a traditional org chart to dismantle "traditional ways of thinking" and force camaraderie in the struggle.
      • Massive Under-hiring: By hiring 40 people to do 80 people’s worth of work, the organization forces employees to abandon arrogance and think from first principles.
      • The Flow State: Like SpaceX and OpenAI, the goal is to create high-pressure environments that strip away ego and focus purely on "what can we do better?"
  • The Recruitment Profile: He seeks individuals focused on their own "journey to resilience," preferring those who have no "priors or bias" from older enterprise software eras.

Future Trends: Distributed Compute and Network Effects

  • The Network Effect of Code: Palihapitiya envisions a future where disparate industries (e.g., a hospital and an aerospace company) share a library of underlying code patterns.
      • Cross-Industry Cooperation: By recompiling code through different "ontologies," the N-plus-first company can leverage the "secrets" of all companies that came before it, creating logarithmic abundance.
  • Open-Weight vs. Closed-Source: He highlights a critical distinction between "closed-source" (US) and "open-weight" (China) models.
      • The Danger of "Vassal States": If five entities control AI, everyone else becomes a "vassal state."
      • Distributed Infrastructure: He advocates for unregulated, distributed forms of compute and inference (e.g., BitTensor) to ensure AI has no "kill switch" and remains de-levered from government oversight.
  • The Positive-Sum Compact: Palihapitiya rejects AI doomerism and the narrative of replacing workers. He advocates for a "methodical" approach where AI helps humans do more, works within regulations, and increases the value of the workforce.

Leadership and Personal Resilience

  • The Fallacy of Failure: Palihapitiya views failure as a social construct used to keep people down. He frames it as "do and learn" rather than an identity.
  • Ego Management: He uses specific "guardrails" to maintain humility:
      • Orthogonal Partners: Surrounding himself with people (like his wife, a pharma CEO) who are unimpressed by ego and focused on practical, high-stakes work.
      • Poker as a Diagnostic: He views poker as a mirror for his mental state; losing streaks often indicate that ego is interfering with judgment.
  • Trauma as a Catalyst: He openly discusses how childhood adversity fueled his initial drive, but emphasizes that long-term success requires evolving past a "less than" mindset toward self-acceptance.
"The ultimate hack for AI is when things are in English. That is the unifying language for eight billion human beings. It is not C, it is not Python... and the space of innovation is not algorithms anymore. It's judgment." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"There is no failure. That is what other people think of you to keep you down. There is no failure... There is do and learn, do and learn, do and learn." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"AI says, 'I am going to learn, I'm going to tokenize that knowledge, and then I'm going to sell subscriptions... I'm going to use that to replace you and fire you.' That is not a positive-sum view... The positive-sum view is we're going to work together." — Chamath Palihapitiya
Thank you guys for coming. Today we have a very special guest who I think needs realistically no introduction, but we're going to introduce him regardless. Chamath is the founder and CEO of 8090, an AI-native company focused on rebuilding and modernizing enterprise solutions. Prior to 8090, Chamath was the founder and CEO of Social Capital, where he backed companies such as Slack, SoFi, Groq, and others. Before that, he was an early executive at Facebook where he scaled the company to the size it is today, and he is a co-host of the All-In Podcast. Chamath, it's a pleasure to have you here today. Thank you for taking the time to join us. Thanks. I went to school with his mom, and I just met his mom. It's all full circle. Small world. All right, so as a brief kind of overview of how the structure's going to go today, guys, we have some questions that we collected that you entered, and then we'll open up the floor to some audience questions. So just if you have any questions, note them down and be ready for them later. So, Jason, you want to kick it off with the first question? Yeah, sure. I guess, like, the first one, you've had a very unconventional career in tech. I think we'd all say, obviously, with the beginnings as an immigrant, and then through Facebook, VC, and then now to founding. Curious just for the broader audience: how did your early life shape the way you think about risk, ownership, and building? Okay, well, first of all, thank you for saying it this way because a lot of people sometimes wonder, like, what's the motivation? I think, like, Kobe Bryant actually had the best answer for this, which is the winning and the losing is sort of like a byproduct of what it feels like to be in the moment. And in the moment, you have the opportunity to practice a craft, get better at something, and mostly prove something to yourself. There are many other ways in which people say that. Some people say that it's how you play an infinite game. Like if you play a finite game, you and I are competing for the scarce resource of a promotion, or for venture capital, or for some external validation, some award, some title. And that it's finite, right? And that's a proposition where if I'm winning, you're losing, and vice-versa. The infinite game is more where you're just like—it's like this constant trajectory. What I've accidentally stumbled into is an infinite game, whereas where I started was playing finite games, where I was competing with everybody around me. So now if you ask me what my motivation is, I think that I've done a couple of really interesting things. When I was an executive, I was mostly asked by somebody else, in that case Zuck, to take on a task. And I had to be a trusted steward, but fundamentally it was his show and I was a supporting actor. And then I left, and then I started an investment business, which was a completely orthogonal thing. At Facebook, I was really successful, I think, and probably could have just stopped. But when I moved—when I went to Social Capital, I had to kind of find a way of betting on other people and managing the capital of other people. When you take money from other people, I don't know about how you guys would react to it, but it was profoundly stressful for me. The people that gave me money, like Mayo Clinic or the pension fund of XYZ, I felt so bad about this idea that I could lose money for these folks. And so I really took that job seriously and then I wanted to prove what it meant to take risk and be an investor. I did that job well. The great thing about 8090 is that it's my first real shot—I've started many projects, I've gotten them off the ground, I'll be an executive chairman—but it's my first real shot at doing the one thing that I've never done. I started something from scratch, the white paper, clean sheet design: "This is what I think we should do," and then build it. And I need to see if I can help make it successful. And if I do that, I feel like I will have done everything professionally that is a challenge. How do you build a trillion-dollar company? Check. How do you build a multi-billion dollar investor firm? Check. Can you start and scale and create something that's many billions? I don't know. But the reason I can be motivated is that I'm framing it as a challenge of who I am as a person. Long-winded answer, but it is such a key to your success. I wish I knew that when I was younger. So many times I blew myself up. So many times I self-sabotaged myself. So many times I just—I fucked up the core essence of what the opportunity was. And the problem with places like this is it can really trick you. I went to an equivalent place like this, not nearly as prestigious, but still quite prestigious in Canada, called the University of Waterloo. You're surrounded by the smartest kids. In that case it's Canadian for the most part, and from, you know, Hong Kong. That's what it was. Canadians and Hong Kong. But they're little geniuses. And when you're surrounded by geniuses, it's easy to get very outwardly focused and competitive with one another. And it is a huge distraction. So you were saying earlier that you shifted from this finite reward system to this infinite reward system where instead of competing with other people for finite resources, you're now on your own lane, if I'm understanding correctly. What were some of the key kind of realizations or practices? Like, how do you—how do you get to that stage? And I feel like a lot of us here grew up in very similar circumstances. You have to run into the wall really hard at high speed. Maybe there are people that are more evolved than I am. I think women are more evolved than maybe they can realize. Men are stupid, so you're going—you have to—you have to just really run into it face-first. And the reason is because one of the most shocking things that you can do is not have an org chart. We don't have an org chart. And so, you know, you drop these people into this soup. It's already stressful. That's one way. There's nobody to—so, you know, we have like nice, kind people that are also struggling. And so there's camaraderie in the struggle and they're willing to help you, but they have just as little of a fucking clue as you do. But that's—there's something beautiful in that, because now you, again, you have to just dismantle all of this traditional way of thinking. Two, you under-hire massively. If you have 80 humans' worth of work, having 40 is an incredibly interesting observational experiment. What happens? And now you combine it with this other pressure: there is no hierarchy to help you. What happens? And eventually what you see is all of these traditional things melt away. Traditional answers to questions melt away. "If this, then that," all melts away, and all of a sudden people are forced to just think from first principles. That is a beautiful place to be, especially early in your career. That's where the magic happens. You become a wholly different person. So I tell people, if you find one of these places—Anthropic is such a place, OpenAI is such a place, Facebook was such a place, Google was such a place, maybe there's pockets of that that are like that now—SpaceX is such a place. And you get into those places because it takes you into that flow state. There's so much work, there's so much pressure, but it strips away the traditional thinking. You have no time for arrogance. Like if you talk to Mark Juncosa at SpaceX, one of the most humble human beings, and you'd be like, "How the fuck is this guy?" and he's like, "What can we do better?" I was with somebody this weekend and we were just commenting on Mark. It's always the question: "What can I do better? What is SpaceX not doing well enough?" When you're a $200 billion company and the CTO asks those kinds of questions, it's a living embodiment of what it means to be in that flow state. But could you imagine the stuff that he is juggling, the pressure that he must be under? And so he has no time to be arrogant. That's what you want. You want to find places like that and you want to run to those places. They're incredibly challenging in some other ways, but they're incredibly unique. Just want to keep us on track. You mentioned 8090, this is obviously like a newer company of yours. What was kind of the rationale, like what was the catalyst of why you started 8090? What was something that you saw, some inefficiency that made you realize that "I'm not going to start another SaaS app" or something? So the way that my mind works is I always think in terms of is there a framework and a set of rules that allow me to abstract what is happening and give it a context. The most important context in AI is all the way back to how the internet initially developed. And the most important context of that is this thing called the OSI reference model. If you just Google that very quickly on your phone, what you're going to see is this image of a seven-layer cake. And at every layer, what's the most important thing to acknowledge is at every layer of that stack, you have seen hundreds of billions to trillions of dollars of value created. So even back then, when those people were imagining what the layers of abstraction would be, they got it so right. That thing was so valuable to me because I used to refer to that all the time when I would meet a new company and I was thinking, "Is this an interesting company?" I would go back to this OSI model and I would say, "Does this logically fit in any one of these things? Where does it fit? What will it do?" It wasn't the, you know, ultimate question, but it was a very important starting point. So I have a framework of that for AI. And in that framework, I spent the first number of years purely in the silicon. Helped this thing called Groq get off the ground. And initially how 8090 started was I thought that what we needed to do was essentially a transpiler, where you could take essentially CUDA and allow it to get to Trainium, Inferentia, AMD, all of this stuff. That's how it initially started. But it turned out to be wrong. I had spent like a bunch of years working and helping the team at Groq. Then I spent a handful of years completely pivoting and doing what I call the fulcrum assets of AI. What are those? It's prismatic LFP and it's actuation. Why? Because if you look inside of a digital AI stack, the most important thing that it lacks is power. The most critical thing for power is both the power generation and the power storage. The power storage, fundamentally iron—where's the OpenAI guy? I mean, where's the Anthropic guy? He was here. Iron, and then you'll see—it'll be prismatic LFP. So I went and helped start a bunch of things there. If you look at physical AI, the biggest Achilles' heel that people will have is the actual actuation. You know, how do you get the sensors to work? How do you kind of pass the "numb-match test" as they call it? How do you do that? The only answer for that is rare earths. And so I spent a bunch of time in that. Then when I came back, back to my reference model, I was looking around, started 8090, thought it was a transpilation problem, realized very quickly it was not. And then it kind of occurred: we have done so much—and I hate to offend anybody here when I say this—we have done so much in AI, okay? We have. But we are training models to understand patterns that we have seen. We do not think from first principles. It's token and then the prediction of the next token, prediction of the next token. I think that that's—it's still an incredible leap that we've created, or that OpenAI, Anthropic, xAI, that they have created. But what is missing? What's missing is being able to translate that in two dimensions. Dimension number one is long-horizon tasks are fundamentally still a joke. It doesn't work. And I don't care what anybody says, don't show me a stupid eval, don't tell me about some dumb script you ran for 48 hours. That's all bullshit. Long-horizon tasks are not well-handled. They just don't work. Second is complex problems also don't work. They are not well-addressed and they're not well-handled. Why is this important? If AI looks like any other technology, we're going to go through the rise, right, the initial hype cycle. We're going to see a natural contraction because somehow, somewhere, something is going to fail. We are all going to see this. And then that's what's called the trough of disillusionment, I think. The MBA folks will confirm if that's true. Then you see the slow, gradual uptake of the real final solution. Happened in the internet, happened before, it's happened in many, many, many cases. The problem with this is that we, though, are spending hundreds and hundreds of billions to trillions of dollars to try to figure out how to cross this chasm. So what do we do? If we don't figure this out, people will hit the trough of disillusionment and say this was a joke. I think we need to be able to take AI into very complicated environments and make it work. So what's my solution to that? I think at a very basic level, you have to have a symbolic space that guides the embedded space. So maybe a little less jargon-y: what is a secret of a company? Like what's the secret inside of Google? What's the secret inside of Uber? What's the secret inside of TikTok? What that is is a symbolic representation. It's when you're in a meeting and somebody says, "Well, you know, here's how we should do the PageRank this way. Here's how we should build GFS in Bigtable." It's all in the symbolic space of English. It's all these like secrets that a company has that describes what they should do. So I was thinking to myself, like, the most valuable thing we could do is build a massive honeypot for the symbolic space, for English. Like for the requirements, for all the secrets, for all of the—like when you guys go work inside a company, at some point, if your company does poorly, they'll bring in McKinsey. And then you'll spend tens of millions of dollars. And what will you get at the end? You'll get a deck, a nice slide deck. What do you do with the deck? Most of the time nothing. And that's the business model. The executives feel better. You'll buy some off-the-shelf piece of software, it will be sold to you as a solution. You'll try to implement it, it really won't work. Then you'll go to a consulting firm, they'll hire hundreds and hundreds of time-and-materials people that look like me—we'll try to implement it, it won't work. And this cruft builds up. So my idea is I want to build basically a control plane for AI. I want to be able to really focus on the things that are the symbolic golden source, the true source, the English-language understanding of what should be done, what is done, what works, what doesn't work. PRDs, requirements. Those are the hard-fought secrets that allow companies to be successful. Code is a mechanistically deterministic thing that either works or doesn't work. So you will get that to perfection well before you get requirements and understanding to perfection. So back to this original problem: when you put trillions of dollars in the ground and someone finally says, "What is the ROI?" you're going to have to point to the economy and say, "These companies are 50% more productive." Unless you completely rebuild how those companies operate, it will not pay that off and there will be blood in the streets. The stock market will just totally turn over on itself. So we have a responsibility to do that. So we have this thing that we call a software factory. You get all these humans that can collaborate with a bunch of very powerful models and you can take all these requirements and you can get them into a very amazing sense of what the scope of what you want to do is, and then we bind it to an engineering plan. You bind that to work orders. Now all of a sudden things can work in the forward pass, the reverse pass. We can take tens of billions of lines of—or tens of millions of lines of codebases, dump them in, and all of a sudden it just propagates backwards and now all of a sudden you have English-language understanding: "This is exactly what this does." And these companies, their eyes are like, "We didn't even know." We have this one customer who's got such an old legacy codebase, they have to go and get retirees to—I swear to God, dude, this is a hundred-billion-dollar-a-year company—out of retirement to come into the office to explain what this COBOL code does. And none of you geniuses were able to figure that out, and nobody before you was able to figure it out, and it turns out that these kinds of problems are the thing that's stopping ROI on trillions of dollars of investment. It's all the hidden context. Hidden context, tribal knowledge. So Peter Thiel calls it like—all great companies are built around secrets. And I think an interesting thought challenge is: what happens if all these secrets get documented? What happens if there's like—so you guys live in a world where GitHub is like the ground truth. Well, instead of GitHub, what if there was an actual set of wiki documents in plain language that the CEO could actually read, that were the actual secrets? You know, what if there was a document that Uber had that actually described surge pricing in English, not in code? And when you manipulate the English-language understanding of the rule, the actual surge pricing algorithm then changes downstream to hundreds of millions of people? What if that were possible? You start to unlock a level of optimization and improvement because you don't have to be technical anymore, you just have to have good judgment and be smart. There's way more people that have good judgment and are smart than are technical. So this is the whole point. Like when technology works, you're opening the aperture. Like when I was in college, I had to learn how to code in C and C++. And you're manipulating memory at this ultra-low level. And then at some point, PHP shows up, and then Python shows up, and JavaScript shows up, and you start to abstract up. And what happened? More of you knew how to do it. Many, many more of you, like Python, raise your hand? Okay, most of the room. C++ raise your hand? Oh bullshit, come on—stop that. Come on, stop. I'm trying to make a point here, guys. Unfortunately, the CS core Stanford class is C++. Okay, well that's smart because you need to understand memory and memory structures. So I get that. But for most people, the point I'm trying to make—you might get more people with C, or maybe assembly. Okay, how many of you know assembly? There's a few. Oh my god, Jesus Christ. But the point I'm trying to make is as the languages get abstracted, the N, the number of people that can participate in that ecosystem goes up. And so the ultimate hack for AI is when things are in English. That is the unifying language for eight billion human beings. It is not C, it is not Python, it is none of these languages here. It's this language here and the space of innovation are not algorithms anymore. It's judgment. It's really good ideas. And that many, many, many more people have. I think touching on this, there is an interesting analogy that said like when the first compiler was invented, there was a lot of kind of uncertainty around with this new tool, right? Like, you would have this code, you'd write it, and then pass it through the compiler, but you weren't sure that like the assembly that it generated was actually correct. And I think we're in like this kind of similar stage with AI, right? Like we have this grand vision, we want to reason on the symbolic level, but in order to do that we have to pass it through kind of this software factory as you're describing, and there are all these kind of like lossy conversions that can happen. And so you mentioned earlier as well, like these agents—there's so much hype around like, "Oh, like Open Claude running for 48 hours and solved my life" or something crazy like that. It's clearly not true, it's clearly an exaggeration and one of the big reasons of this is, as you were saying, there's a lot of secrets, right? There's landmines, you have to bring in the retired developer from 15 years ago to explain why something happened and explain why ChatGPT deleted this line and now the whole codebase is broken. So in this path towards this ultimate goal, right, we want to reason in English, what are some of the biggest challenges that you guys are facing that you think more people need to be thinking about? So one is a technical challenge and the other one is one of business. The technical challenge is we're struggling with the same things that everybody else struggles with: what is the interplay and the interface between where the agent starts and ends and where the humans get involved? How do you actually create the right evals and the right guardrails for the agents to stay on task? How do you actually create a knowledge graph that these agents can traverse so that they can stay at the level of embeddings and actually be able to act over reasonably long-horizon tasks? So those are a set of technical challenges. And that creates really interesting questions of business strategy. You know, we had a very important conversation today which is: what is the value of a control plane? And won't we just be seeding ourselves to Cursor, as an example, which is a phenomenal product? And what you're having to navigate there is just, you know, in my language and my understanding, I would rather build MS-DOS and Windows, not necessarily Adobe Photoshop. And in this context, I think that for AI we don't have that control plane. That's what I would like to build. I want to have something that basically says the ground source golden truth for all of these agents downstream will always be this hardware-independent, database-independent, language-independent symbolic representation of what you want to do. So go and convince business people of that—they get that. So we can sell the shit out of that. But then translating that into a set of agents that can act on target and on task is very, very difficult. It's a very complicated problem. The business challenge is then to convince them why those innovations should stay with us. What do I mean by that? So now I go back to: how do you build a trillion-dollar company? What was the term? Network effect. What is a network effect that has never been built before? There's never been a network effect built around these kinds of secrets. Around code. Meaning: if I was a hospital and I'm trying to diagnose a cancer patient, or I'm an airplane company designing a new wing, immediately where your brain goes to is those are two totally different sets of challenges, two totally different sets of problems. And what I would say is wrong. I challenge you to say the following: that may be a different ontology, but it's probably the same problem. And at the level of code and at the level of assembly, it all looks the same. You agree? So what if it were actually possible for this company to be able to leverage that code because it's just recompiled with a different ontology so that you understand it? Now all of a sudden you have a network effect. Now the N-plus-first company gets to leverage all the secrets of the N companies before it. So when Elon talks about abundance, that's how I translate it. That's how you create just a logarithmic expansion of abundance. You can do anything quickly, cheaply. So we are navigating this very complicated business path to convince these businesses to let us do that with them. To convince them why this is important. Now that's a unique task. But if we accomplish it, we are giving all of these technical people that work at 8090 a chance to do something very profound. If we don't have that, it's a wonderful business. We'll do the same thing as everybody else does. We'll raise money, it'll be a unicorn, then there'll be a decacorn, blah, blah, blah, whatever. But that's not what I care about. I want to prove this other thing. There's a network effect in the code. That businesses can have a shared cooperative approach to this thing. That there is no reason why Boeing needs to be afraid of Memorial Sloan Kettering. In fact, it's the opposite: Boeing and Memorial Sloan Kettering can sort of cooperate and both can thrive. Their costs go down, their value goes up, their downstream impact on their customers and patients goes up. That is a positive-sum view of AI. There has to be some sort of initial apprehension, though, with these companies sharing my secrets, right? Huge. It's enormous. How do you overcome that? That's about trust and reputation. The thing that we do is we methodically—first of all, so this is more about business than technology. In business, it's very important to sort of fish where the fish are, which is to say that in every technology adoption curve, you'll always go through the same transition. You have the early adopters, then you have this sort of mass middle, and then you have laggards. Historically there is a very pejorative or negative way in which you view those three classes, particularly the last two classes. And I've challenged these assumptions to say something different, which is everybody has a different risk posture. It's not—I actually think it's very unfair to call folks that wait until the end laggards. If you're a regulated pharmaceutical company—I was reminded of this this weekend—and you do something wrong, you can go to jail. So it's not like they're like, "I want to poo-poo this thing." It's like they have a responsibility to all of you that when you land a drug inside of your body, that it doesn't, you know, grow an arm out your forehead. Do you know what I mean? So that's not being a laggard, that's being responsible for their customers. One. Two, I said this earlier: right now we have a very complicated moment in AI, which is that there is doomerism on one side and full-throttle capitalism for me on the other. Those are the two choices. Like think of the compact of the internet up until AI. The compact of the internet was we would make products where you would participate and contribute, and the quid pro quo was you would get a value that you would assess as being greater than what you are giving me. You use Facebook and Instagram, you take photos. Well, thank you very much, it allows us to create a network effect. We're able to build a trillion-dollar company, you still find value. Now think of what AI says. AI says, "I am going to learn, I'm going to tokenize that knowledge, and then I'm going to sell subscriptions. Thank you very much, see you later." That is not a positive-sum view, nor is the one that says, "I'm going to go and then use that to replace you and fire you." That is deeply and fundamentally irresponsible. The positive-sum view is we're going to work together, we're going to take our time and methodically navigate this complexity. We're going to actually show how you're hiring more people, you're paying them more. That's how we do it. So it's slow, it's methodical, but it's really working. Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth gives update on US Iran ceasefire

WGXA(11.6K) ·27:09 ·109 views · 2026-05-05
Pete Hegseth (Defense Secretary)
The United States has launched "Project Freedom," a temporary defensive operation to secure the Strait of Hormuz and restore the free flow of global commerce amidst a fragile ceasefire with Iran.
TL;DR The United States has launched "Project Freedom," a temporary defensive operation to secure the Strait of Hormuz and restore the free flow of global commerce amidst a fragile ceasefire with Iran. While maintaining an ironclad blockade on Iranian ports, the U.S. is...

TL;DR

The United States has launched "Project Freedom," a temporary defensive operation to secure the Strait of Hormuz and restore the free flow of global commerce amidst a fragile ceasefire with Iran. While maintaining an ironclad blockade on Iranian ports, the U.S. is providing a "security dome" for international shipping, having already successfully escorted the first commercial vessels through the waterway.

Mission Objectives and Scope: Project Freedom

  • Core Purpose: Directed by President Trump, Project Freedom aims to protect innocent commercial shipping from Iranian aggression and "international extortion" in the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Operational Distinction: Secretary Pete Hegseth emphasized that this mission is separate from *Operation Epic Fury*. It is characterized as:
      • Defensive in nature: Focused on protection rather than starting new combat.
      • Limited in scope: U.S. forces do not need to enter Iranian waters or airspace.
      • Temporary in duration: The U.S. expects partner nations to take over responsibility "soon," as the waterway is more vital to global interests than strictly U.S. interests.
  • Countering Iranian Tactics: The mission specifically targets Iran’s attempt to impose an illegal "tolling system" and its harassment of civilian vessels.

Tactical Execution and Military Presence

  • The "Security Dome": General Caine described a 24/7 overwatch consisting of a "red, white, and blue dome" over the southern side of the strait.
  • Force Composition:
      • Personnel: Over 15,000 American service members are currently deployed to the mission.
      • Assets: The operation includes U.S. Navy guided-missile destroyers, supported by over 100 fighter jets, attack aircraft, helicopters (MH-60s), and AH-64 Apache helicopters.
      • The 82nd Airborne Division: Acting as the core of the immediate response force, paratroopers are synchronizing land, air, sea, and cyber forces using AI-enabled tactical networks to manage command and control.
  • Initial Successes: Two U.S.-flagged commercial ships have already successfully transited the strait. Currently, hundreds of ships are lining up to follow, seeking exit from the "Iranian trap."

Iranian Aggression and the Global Blockade

  • Ongoing Hostilities: Despite the ceasefire, General Caine reported that Iran has fired at commercial vessels nine times, seized two container ships, and attacked U.S. forces over 10 times—all characterized as being below the threshold of restarting major combat.
  • Regional Instability: In the last 24 hours, Iran launched attacks against Oman (once) and the UAE (three times), including a defeated attack on the Fujairah oil terminal.
  • The U.S. Blockade: While opening the strait to international traffic, the U.S. continues an "ironclad blockade" on Iranian ports.
      • Enforcement: Six ships attempted to run the blockade at the start of Project Freedom and were successfully turned back.
  • Economic Impact: Iran’s actions have effectively "trapped" 22,500 mariners on more than 1,550 commercial vessels in the Arabian Gulf, weaponizing a choke point that handles one-fifth of global oil consumption.

Diplomatic Strategy and Future Outlook

  • Ceasefire Status: Secretary Hegseth clarified that the ceasefire is not over, despite the "churn" of initial kinetic engagements. The U.S. remains "locked and loaded" to resume major combat operations if Iran violates the core terms.
  • Strategic Goals: The administration’s primary objectives remain preventing Iran from obtaining a nuclear weapon and ensuring freedom of navigation.
  • International Participation: The U.S. is calling on allies and partner nations with an "equity stake" in the strait to prepare to assume the burden of security.
  • War Powers Compliance: Hegseth noted that with the ceasefire, the "60-day War Powers clock" has stopped, but the President retains the authority to restart operations if Iran fails to follow through on its side of the bargain.
"The world needs this waterway a lot more than we do. We're stabilizing the situation so commerce can flow again, but we expect the world to step up. At the appropriate time and soon, we will hand responsibility back to you." — Secretary Pete Hegseth
"No adversary should mistake our current restraint with a lack of resolve... CENTCOM and the rest of the joint force remain ready to resume major combat operations against Iran if ordered to do so." — General Caine
"Iran is the clear aggressor, harassing civilian vessels, threatening mariners from every nation indiscriminately, and weaponizing a critical choke point for its own financial benefit." — Secretary Pete Hegseth
Well, as you know, President Trump has directed US Central Command to restart the free flow of commerce through the Strait of Hormuz under the umbrella of Project Freedom. To be clear, this operation is separate and distinct from Operation Epic Fury. Project Freedom is defensive in nature, focused in scope, and temporary in duration, with one mission: protecting innocent commercial shipping from Iranian aggression. American forces won't need to enter Iranian waters or airspace. It's not necessary. We're not looking for a fight, but Iran also cannot be allowed to block innocent countries and their goods from an international waterway. Iran is the clear aggressor, harassing civilian vessels, threatening mariners from every nation indiscriminately, and weaponizing a critical choke point for its own financial benefit—or at least trying to. For too long, Iran has been harassing ships, shooting at civilian tankers from all nations, and trying to impose a tolling system. Iran's plan, a form of international extortion, is unacceptable. That ends with Project Freedom. Two US commercial ships, along with American destroyers, have already safely transited the strait, showing the lane is clear. We know the Iranians are embarrassed by this fact. They said they controlled the strait; they do not. So, American ships led the way—commercial and military—shouldering the initial risk from the front, as Americans always do. And right now, hundreds more ships from nations around the world are lining up to transit. CENTCOM, along with partner nations, is in active communication with hundreds of ships, shipping companies, and insurers. All of these ships from all around the world want to get out of the Iranian trap that they have been stuck inside. Except Iran's, of course. Which is why our ironclad blockade remains in full effect as well. On Sunday, at the direction of the President, CENTCOM initiated Project Freedom with the objective of facilitating the safe passage of international commercial shipping through the Strait of Hormuz. Over the past seven weeks, Iran has repeatedly threatened and attacked commercial shipping in the strait in order to effectively cut off commercial traffic and damage the global economy. The 82nd is the core of our nation's immediate response force and ready to deploy within hours of notice. These paratroopers are capable of accomplishing any mission in any environment, constantly training, constantly ready to jump from Air Force aircraft into ground combat and seize key terrain if ordered to do so. This is just like their predecessors did in Sicily and Normandy in World War II, or to secure or enable the follow-on forces to flow into theater as they did in Grenada or Panama. They can do the range of missions from counter-insurgency to counter-terrorism operations to power projection, as they have many times over the course of the history of this great division. They can do all these things because they're incredibly well-trained, well-equipped, and well-led, but also because of the indomitable spirit of the All-American paratrooper. Every single one of them volunteered for this role, volunteered to go to airborne training, and volunteered to serve. But this division is now more than just a formation that we use for forcible entries. For example, just today, they are out there coordinating, integrating, and synchronizing land, air, space, sea, and cyber forces using all joint all-domain command and control. They use next-generation tactical networks that are AI-enabled to augment intelligence and operations, and they seamlessly synchronize all of these effects and are doing so in support of Project Freedom as we speak. The relentless focus on technical innovation allows the commanders out there in the field to see, sense, and understand the opportunities that are in front of them, along with the associated risks, and make smart decisions out on the objective. With incredible courage, tenacity, and grit, they are going all the way every day, and whenever the nation calls on them, they are there to answer the call. We are grateful for their service and their leadership. Lastly, before I close as I often do, I want to remember some of our fallen. Today we remember Sergeant William Rivers, Specialist Brianna Moffatt, and Specialist Kennedy Sanders of the 718th Engineering Company, US Army Reserves, out of Fort Benning, Georgia. They were tragically killed on January 28, 2024, when attacked by an Iranian-backed proxy force at Tower 22 in Jordan. We continue to carry on their memories; we remember their names, and we remember their families.
